/**\n45:  * Container for connectors which is responsible for managing their lifecycle (e.g. handling startup,\n46:  * shutdown, pausing, etc.). Internally, we manage the runtime state of the connector and transition according\n47:  * to target state changes. For example, in distributed mode, an implementation of this interface\\n45:  * knows how to accept a connector configuration, may need to route it to the current leader worker for the cluster so\\n46:  * the config can be written to persistent storage, and then ensures the new connector is correctly instantiated on one\\n47:  * of the workers.\\n48:  * </p>\\n49:  * <p>\\n50:  * This class must implement all the actions that can be taken on the cluster (add/remove connectors, pause/resume tasks,\\n51:  * get state of connectors and tasks, etc). the generalized\\n134:  *     group/consumer coordinator). Each instance of DistributedHerder joins the group and indicates what its current\\n135:  *     configuration state is (where it is in the configuration log). The group coordinator selects one member to take\\n136:  *     this information and assign each instance a subset of the active connectors & tasks to execute. The assignment\\n137:  *     strategy depends on the {@link ConnectAssignor} used. Once an assignment is received, the DistributedHerder simply\\n138:  *     runs its assigned connectors and tasks in a {@link Worker}.\\n139:  * </p>\\n140:  * <p>\\n141:  *     In addition to distributing work, the DistributedHerder uses the leader determined during the work assignment\\n142:  *     to select a leader for this generation of the group who is responsible for other tasks that can only be performed\\n143:  *     by a single node at a time. Most importantly, this includes writing updated configurations for connectors and tasks,\\n144:  *     (and therefore, also for creating, destroy, and scaling up/down connectors).\\n145:  * </p>\\n146:  * <p>\\n147:  *     The DistributedHerder uses a single thread for most of its processing. The latter are pushed\\n149:  *     into a queue until the thread has time to handle them. A consequence of this is that requests can get blocked\\n150:  *     behind a worker rebalance. org.apache.kafka.connect.runtime.distributed.IncrementalCooperativeConnectProtocol.CONNECT_PROTOCOL_V2;\n127: \n128: /**\n129:  * <p>\n130:  *     Distributed \"herder\" that coordinates with other workers to spread work across multiple processes.\n131:  * </p>\n132:  * <p>\n133:  *     Under the hood, this is implemented as a group managed by Kafka's group membership facilities (i.e. the generalized\n134:  *     group/consumer coordinator). Each instance of DistributedHerder joins the group and indicates what its current\n135:  *     configuration state is (where it is in the configuration log). The group coordinator selects one member to take\n136:  *     this information and assign each instance a subset of the active connectors & tasks to execute. The assignment\n137:  *     strategy depends on the {@link ConnectAssignor} used. Once an assignment is received, the DistributedHerder simply\n138:  *     runs its assigned connectors and tasks in a {@link Worker}.\n139:  * </p>\n140:  * <p>\n141:  *     In addition to distributing work, the DistributedHerder uses the leader determined during the work assignment\n142:  *     to select a leader for this generation of the group who is responsible for other tasks that can only be performed\n143:  *     by a single node at a time. Most importantly, this includes writing updated configurations for connectors and tasks,\n144:  *     (and therefore, also for creating, destroy, and scaling up/down connectors).\n145:  * </p>\n146:  * <p>\n147:  *     The DistributedHerder uses a single thread for most of its processing. This includes processing\n148:  *     config changes, handling task rebalances and serving requests from the HTTP layer. The latter are pushed\n149:  *     into a queue until the thread has time to handle them. A consequence of this is that requests can get blocked\n150:  *     behind a worker rebalance. When the herder knows that a rebalance is expected, it typically returns an error\n151:  *     immediately to the request, but this is not always possible (in particular when another worker has requested\n152:  *     the rebalance). Similar to handling HTTP requests, config changes which are observed asynchronously by polling\n153:  *     the config log are batched for handling in the work thread.\n154:  * </p>\n155:  */\n156: All the \\\"real\\\" work of the herder is\\n414:         // performed in this thread, which keeps synchronization straightforward at the cost of some operations possibly\\n415:         // blocking up this thread (especially those in callbacks due to rebalance events).\\n416: \\n417:         try {\\n418:             // if we failed to read to end of log before, we need to make sure the issue was resolved before joining group\\n419:             // Joining and immediately leaving for failure to read configs is exceedingly impolite\\n420:             if (!canReadConfigs) {\\n421:                 if (readConfigToEnd(workerSyncTimeoutMs)) {\\n422:                     canReadConfigs = true;\\n423:                 } else {\\n424:                     return; // Safe to return and tick immediately because readConfigToEnd will do the backoff for us\\n425:                 }\\n426:             }\\n427: \\n428:             log.debug(\\\"Ensuring group membership is still active\\\");\\n429:             String stageDescription = \\\"ensuring membership in the cluster\\\";\\n430:             member.ensureActive(() -> new TickThreadStage(stageDescription));\\n431:             completeTickThreadStage();\\n432:             // Ensure we're in a good state in our group. leader. All the \"real\" work of the herder is\n414:         // performed in this thread, which keeps synchronization straightforward at the cost of some operations possibly\n415:         // blocking up this thread (especially those in callbacks due to rebalance events).\n416: \n417:         try {\n418:             // if we failed to read to end of log before, we need to make sure the issue was resolved before joining group\n419:             // Joining and immediately leaving for failure to read configs is exceedingly impolite\n420:             if (!canReadConfigs) {\n421:                 if (readConfigToEnd(workerSyncTimeoutMs)) {\n422:                     canReadConfigs = true;\n423:                 } else {\n424:                     return; // Safe to return and tick immediately because readConfigToEnd will do the backoff for us\n425:                 }\n426:             }\n427: \n428:             log.debug(\"Ensuring group membership is still active\");\n429:             String stageDescription = \"ensuring membership in the cluster\";\n430:             member.ensureActive(() -> new TickThreadStage(stageDescription));\n431:             completeTickThreadStage();\n432:             // Ensure we're in a good state in our group. The intuition is that there should\\n118:  *    be less chance of write conflicts when the worker has just received its assignment and is starting tasks.\\n119:  *    In particular, this prevents us from depending on the generation absolutely. Now I have a comprehensive understanding of the lifecycle pattern in Kafka. Let me now create the answer.json file with all the findings. I've found:\n\n1. **Core Lifecycle Interface and Classes:**\n   - `Herder.java` - interface with start() and stop() methods\n   - `AbstractHerder.java` - base implementation with lifecycle management\n   - `DistributedHerder.java` - distributed implementation with coordinated lifecycle\n   \n2. **Storage/Backing Store Interfaces:**\n   - `OffsetBackingStore.java` - interface with start/stop\n   - `StatusBackingStore.java` - interface with start/stop\n   - `ConfigBackingStore.java` - interface with start/stop\n   \n3. **Storage/Backing Store Implementations:**\n   - `KafkaOffsetBackingStore.java` - Kafka-backed offset store\n   - `MemoryOffsetBackingStore.java` - in-memory implementation\n   - `FileOffsetBackingStore.java` - file-backed implementation\n   - `KafkaStatusBackingStore.java` - Kafka-backed status store (needs to find)\n   - `MemoryStatusBackingStore.java` - in-memory status store\n   - `KafkaConfigBackingStore.java` - Kafka-backed config store (needs to find)\n   - `MemoryConfigBackingStore.java` - in-memory config store\n\n4. **REST Server Lifecycle:**\n   - `RestServer.java` - abstract REST server with lifecycle management\n   - `ConnectRestServer.java` - Connect REST server implementation\n   \n5. **Top-level Orchestrator:**\n   - `Connect.java` - ties together herder, worker, and REST server lifecycle\n   \n6. **Key Lifecycle Patterns:**\n   - Services are started in order: worker -> statusBackingStore -> configBackingStore (in AbstractHerder.startServices)\n   - DistributedHerder initiates group coordination and loads configuration from topics during startup. For example, KafkaOffsetBackingStore.start() (line 231) initializes the KafkaBasedLog for reading/writing offsets.\\n\\n**REST Server Lifecycle:**\\n\\nThe `RestServer` class (connect/runtime/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/connect/runtime/rest/RestServer.java) manages the HTTP control plane:\\n- `initializeServer()`: Starts the Jetty server and initializes handlers\\n- `stop()`: Gracefully shuts down handlers, REST extensions, and the Jetty server with a 60-second timeout\\n\\n**Key Design Patterns:**\\n\\n1. **Dependency Ordering**: Services are started in order of their dependencies, and stopped in reverse order. This ensures dependent services are ready before dependents are initialized.\\n\\n2. **Graceful Shutdown**: Shutdown operations use configurable timeouts (e.g., GRACEFUL_SHUTDOWN_TIMEOUT_MS = 60 seconds in RestServer) to allow in-flight requests to complete.\\n\\n3. **Coordinated Shutdown**: DistributedHerder coordinates shutdown across distributed nodes using the group membership protocol, ensuring proper cleanup.\\n\\n4. **Lifecycle Hooks**: `startServices()` and `stopServices()` serve as extension points for subclasses to customize initialization/shutdown.\\n\\n5. **Resource Cleanup**: The `uponShutdown` list in DistributedHerder allows external components to register cleanup actions that execute at shutdown time.\\n\\n**MirrorMaker Integration:**\\n\\nMirrorMaker (connect/mirror/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/connect/mirror/MirrorMaker.java) demonstrates the lifecycle pattern at a higher level, managing multiple herders and coordinating their startup/shutdown.\\n\\nThis lifecycle management ensures that Kafka Connect can reliably initialize complex, distributed systems while guaranteeing that all resources are properly cleaned up during shutdown, preventing resource leaks and data loss.\"\n}\n"},"caller":{"type":"direct"}}],"stop_reason":null,"stop_sequence":null,"usage":{"input_tokens":11,"cache_creation_input_tokens":4960,"cache_read_input_tokens":106502,"cache_creation":{"ephemeral_5m_input_tokens":0,"ephemeral_1h_input_tokens":4960},"output_tokens":1,"service_tier":"standard","inference_geo":"not_available"},"context_management":null},"parent_tool_use_id":null,"session_id":"87170f46-23bb-450e-a407-bce2d5d1f2a4","uuid":"4e366567-2186-4c60-8a59-13db3a36e84b"}

The architecture follows a hierarchical service initialization and coordinated shutdown pattern.\\n\\n**Core Lifecycle Architecture:**\\n\\nThe lifecycle pattern is built around the `Herder` interface (connect/runtime/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/connect/runtime/Herder.java), which defines `start()` and `stop()` methods. This interface represents the main service coordinator that manages all components of a Kafka Connect worker.\\n\\n**Lifecycle Orchestration Layers:**\\n\\n1. **Top-Level Orchestrator (Connect.java)**: The `Connect` class serves as the primary entry point, managing the lifecycle of three key components:\\n   - The Herder (coordinator of connectors/tasks)\\n   - REST server for the control plane\\n   - A shutdown hook for graceful shutdown\\n   \\n   Startup sequence: `herder.start()` → `rest.initializeResources()`\\n   Shutdown sequence: `rest.stop()` → `herder.stop()` (reverse order)\\n\\n2. **Herder Implementation Hierarchy:**\\n   - **AbstractHerder** (connect/runtime/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/connect/runtime/AbstractHerder.java): Provides the base lifecycle management with `startServices()` and `stopServices()` template methods.\\n   - **DistributedHerder** (connect/runtime/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/connect/runtime/distributed/DistributedHerder.java): Extends AbstractHerder with distributed coordination.\\n\\n**Service Initialization Sequence (Forward):**\\n\\nIn `AbstractHerder.startServices()` (lines 180-184):\\n1. `this.worker.start()` - Initializes the worker that runs connectors and tasks\\n2. `this.statusBackingStore.start()` - Starts the status storage service\\n3. `this.configBackingStore.start()` - Starts the configuration storage service\\n\\nIn `DistributedHerder.run()` (lines 376-403), after startServices():\\n1. Reads to the end of internal topics\\n2. Joins the consumer group\\n3. Waits for initial rebalance\\n4. Begins processing external requests and rebalance events\\n\\n**Service Shutdown Sequence (Reverse Order):**\\n\\nIn `AbstractHerder.stopServices()` (lines 186-192):\\n1. `this.statusBackingStore.stop()` - Stops status storage (first dependency)\\n2. `this.configBackingStore.stop()` - Stops configuration storage\\n3. `this.worker.stop()` - Stops the worker (last, was started first)\\n4. `this.connectorExecutor.shutdown()` - Shuts down executor service\\n5. Closes connector client config override policy plugin\\n\\nIn `DistributedHerder.stop()` (lines 852-862):\\n1. Sets stopping flag\\n2. Wakes up group member\\n3. Shuts down herderExecutor with graceful timeout\\n4. Shuts down forwardRequestExecutor\\n5. Shuts down startAndStopExecutor\\n\\nIn `DistributedHerder.closeResources()` (lines 838-842):\\n1. Stops worker group member\\n2. Closes herder metrics\\n3. Closes any custom resources managed via uponShutdown\\n\\n**Backing Store Lifecycle (Storage Interfaces):**\\n\\nThree backing store interfaces follow the standard lifecycle pattern:\\n\\n- **OffsetBackingStore** (connect/runtime/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/connect/storage/OffsetBackingStore.java): Manages connector offsets\\n  - Implementations: KafkaOffsetBackingStore, FileOffsetBackingStore, MemoryOffsetBackingStore\\n  \\n- **ConfigBackingStore** (connect/runtime/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/connect/storage/ConfigBackingStore.java): Manages connector and task configurations\\n  - Implementations: KafkaConfigBackingStore, MemoryConfigBackingStore\\n  \\n- **StatusBackingStore** (connect/runtime/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/connect/storage/StatusBackingStore.java): Manages connector and task status information\\n  - Implementations: KafkaStatusBackingStore, MemoryStatusBackingStore\\n\\nAll backing stores follow the pattern where `start()` initializes resources and `stop()` performs graceful shutdown. For example, KafkaOffsetBackingStore.start() (line 231) initializes the KafkaBasedLog for reading/writing offsets.\\n\\n**REST Server Lifecycle:**\\n\\nThe `RestServer` class (connect/runtime/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/connect/runtime/rest/RestServer.java) manages the HTTP control plane:\\n- `initializeServer()`: Starts the Jetty server and initializes handlers\\n- `stop()`: Gracefully shuts down handlers, REST extensions, and the Jetty server with a 60-second timeout\\n\\n**Key Design Patterns:**\\n\\n1. **Dependency Ordering**: Services are started in order of their dependencies, and stopped in reverse order. This ensures dependent services are ready before dependents are initialized.\\n\\n2. **Graceful Shutdown**: Shutdown operations use configurable timeouts (e.g., GRACEFUL_SHUTDOWN_TIMEOUT_MS = 60 seconds in RestServer) to allow in-flight requests to complete.\\n\\n3. **Coordinated Shutdown**: DistributedHerder coordinates shutdown across distributed nodes using the group membership protocol, ensuring proper cleanup.\\n\\n4. **Lifecycle Hooks**: `startServices()` and `stopServices()` serve as extension points for subclasses to customize initialization/shutdown.\\n\\n5. **Resource Cleanup**: The `uponShutdown` list in DistributedHerder allows external components to register cleanup actions that execute at shutdown time.\\n\\n**MirrorMaker Integration:**\\n\\nMirrorMaker (connect/mirror/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/connect/mirror/MirrorMaker.java) demonstrates the lifecycle pattern at a higher level, managing multiple herders and coordinating their startup/shutdown.\\n\\nThis lifecycle management ensures that Kafka Connect can reliably initialize complex, distributed systems while guaranteeing that all resources are properly cleaned up during shutdown, preventing resource leaks and data loss.\"\n}\n","structuredPatch":[],"originalFile":null}}
